What an absolutely wonderful performance. Let's not overlook Finneas' role in Billie Eilish's success, which is of course founded on a technical precision that was honed to perfection in the family home. There's something magical about sibling musical chemistry, based not only a shared genetic heritage, but the close proximity of multiple talents feeding off each other for years. In the case of the O'Connell household the unique circumstance that gives us this amazing performance wasn't only the usual musical background of guitars and pianos, but the incorporation of the laptop and an abundance of creative musical software. It was the technology that let Billie and her brother work the arrangements and sonic production into a state of lucidity that we've really never heard before. That's not to play down the sheer musicality of the duo (in either of their solo careers) but to go some way to explain why it sounds so sharp. It's been forged and honed and tempered in an intimate studio environment (their own home) that has led to the blossoming of what I would call an unprecedented musical and emotional phenomenon. NPR, and The Music media is not covering the biggest story in music, fake vocals: vocals that are autotuned, pitch corrected, or vocals mimed in concert performances. Tony desk, are any vocals fixed at any stage of tiny desk concerts.  These are often applied in remixes. The technology that allows a person to auto tune and pitch correct a voice, is also the technology that proves when a singer is using it.  Here is a partial list of proven performers, caught with fake vocals:  Eagles, Bee Gees, Freddie Mercury, Simon and Garfunkel,  Taylor Swift, Lenny Kravitz, Celine, Pentatonix, Creed, Whitney Houston, Paul McCartney, Pink Floyd, Bryan Adams,  Mariah Carey,  Charli XCX, Michael Buble, Adam Levine, Ariana Grande, the Wicked film,  Sabrina Carpenter,  Sir Cliff Richard, Sir Elton John, Dua Lipa, Chicago, Kelly Clarkson Show, The Voice,  Britain's Got Talent, The X Factor, AMA Awards Show, MTV Awards Show, BTS and most K-Pop groups, The First Take Youtube channel, etc.    This is now an industry standard  practice of all three major labels that control 70% of the music industry directly, Warners,  Universal,  and Sony.    This fakery not only cheats fans but is unfair to artists like Cher, Billie Eilish, Adele, Pink, U2, Carrie Underwood, Lainy Wilson, Pearl Jam, Simply Red, Doja Cat, or Roger Daltry, who don't use it. People need to know when there are fake vocals pretending to be real talent.

I came to this channel looking for a unique recording of Billie Eilish, hoping to find something raw and natural to sing along with in my own process of learning a cappella. What I found was far more than just a performanceâ€”it was a gateway to understanding music and voices on a deeper level. Hearing Billie Eilishâ€™s Tiny Desk performance, produced with such care and precision by Josh Rogosin, felt like stepping into the music itself. Itâ€™s not just a recordingâ€”itâ€™s an intimate experience where every voice, every sound, and every nuance feels alive and personal. This artistry makes Tiny Desk performances so unique, and itâ€™s why I keep coming back.  Tiny Desk isnâ€™t just a platform for music; itâ€™s a masterclass in capturing authenticity and storytelling through sound. Listening to these performances helps me feel more connected to the artists I admire, and it inspires me to push myself further in my own singing journey. Consistently in the last several years, Artists are coming on Tinydesk with arrangements and production that beat the "studio" recordings by a long, long way. Makes me think that by limiting what you can do by what can be physically achieved by a few musicians in a corner of a room, artists, producers and engineers are forced into real focus on what a song truly is, and what's needed to best communicate that - rather than fall back on habit, feeling tied to genre convention, or "filling in the gaps" to make up for incomplete arrangements or less compelling songwriting and/or  performances. I've been producing for about 15 years and have always preferred to build up music layer by layer, but this show is increasingly exposing to me what I may be losing with this approach.

I was probably around 13 or 14 years old at the time. I wish that when I was first told, it had been a joke and that he was never diagnosed with cancer. It was so hard not to break down in front of him and pretend everything was okay. The line â€œThe smile that you gave me even when you felt like dyingâ€fits so well because he always tried to smile and cheer me up, as if he could see right through me and understand how scared I was.   His hospital room faced Central Park, and during my first visit, I couldn't help but look out the window and cry. I had been told not to let him see me sad, but that day, I just couldnâ€™t hold it back. Seeing him in that state, when he had always been so strong, was heartbreakingâ€”it was as if half of the man he was had disappeared. I spent every night, before and after his passing, crying alone because I didnâ€™t know how to share the grief or the pain I felt.
